The **Milky Way** or **Milky Way Galaxy**[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-context-30) is the [galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy") that includes the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), with the name describing the [galaxy's appearance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#Appearance) from [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth"): a hazy band of light seen in the [night sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Night_sky "Night sky") formed from stars in other arms of the galaxy, which are so far away that they cannot be individually distinguished by the [naked eye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naked_eye "Naked eye").

The Milky Way is a [barred spiral galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy "Barred spiral galaxy") with a [D 25 isophotal diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy#Isophotal_diameter "Galaxy") estimated at 26.8±1.1 [kiloparsecs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs "Parsec") (87,400±3,600 [light-years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year "Light-year")),[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Goodwin-11) but only about 1,000 light-years thick at the spiral arms (more at the bulge). Recent simulations suggest that a [dark matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter "Dark matter") area, also containing some visible stars, may extend up to a diameter of almost 2 million light-years (613 kpc).[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-croswell2020-31)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-dearson2020-32) The Milky Way has several [satellite galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Milky_Way%27s_satellite_galaxies "List of Milky Way's satellite galaxies") and is part of the [Local Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Group "Local Group") of galaxies, forming part of the [Virgo Supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster "Virgo Supercluster") which is itself a component of the [Laniakea Supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster "Laniakea Supercluster").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-33)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-34)

It is estimated to contain 100–400 billion stars[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-35)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-36) and at least that number of [planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet").[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Nature-20120111-37)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Space-20130102-38) The Solar System is located at a radius of about 27,000 light-years (8.3 kpc) from the [Galactic Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center"),[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Gillessen2016-39) on the inner edge of the [Orion Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Arm "Orion Arm"), one of the spiral-shaped concentrations of gas and dust. The stars in the innermost 10,000 light-years form a [bulge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy) "Bulge (astronomy)") and one or more bars that radiate from the bulge. The Galactic Center is an intense radio source known as [Sagittarius A*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_A* "Sagittarius A*"), a [supermassive black hole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole "Supermassive black hole") of 4.100 (± 0.034) million [solar masses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass").[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NYT-20220131-40)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ARX-20220128-41) The oldest stars in the Milky Way are nearly as old as the [Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe") itself and thus probably formed shortly after the [Dark Ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe#Dark_Ages "Chronology of the universe") of the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang "Big Bang").[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-HD_140283_(arXiv)-42)

[Galileo Galilei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") first resolved the band of light into individual stars with his telescope in 1610. Until the early 1920s, most astronomers thought that the Milky Way contained all the stars in the Universe.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-43) Following the 1920 [Great Debate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy) "Great Debate (astronomy)") between the astronomers [Harlow Shapley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harlow_Shapley "Harlow Shapley") and [Heber Doust Curtis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heber_Doust_Curtis "Heber Doust Curtis"),[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-shapley_curtis-44) observations by [Edwin Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_Hubble "Edwin Hubble") in 1923 showed that the Milky Way was just one of many galaxies.

Mythology
---------

In the [Babylonian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonia "Babylonia") epic poem _[Enūma Eliš](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/En%C5%ABma\_Eli%C5%A1 "Enūma Eliš")_, the Milky Way is created from the severed tail of the primeval salt water [dragoness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dragon "Dragon")[Tiamat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiamat "Tiamat"), set in the sky by [Marduk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marduk "Marduk"), the Babylonian [national god](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_god "National god"), after slaying her.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-45)[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-46) This story was once thought to have been based on an older [Sumerian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumer "Sumer") version in which Tiamat is instead slain by [Enlil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlil "Enlil") of [Nippur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nippur "Nippur"),[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-47)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lambert1964-48) but is now thought to be purely an invention of Babylonian propagandists with the intention of showing Marduk as superior to the Sumerian deities.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lambert1964-48)

Etymology
---------

In [Greek mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology"), [Zeus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeus "Zeus") places his son born to a mortal woman, the infant [Heracles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heracles "Heracles"), on [Hera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hera "Hera")'s breast while she is asleep so the baby will drink [her divine milk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milk_of_Hera "Milk of Hera") and become immortal. Hera wakes up while breastfeeding and then realizes she is nursing an unknown baby: she pushes the baby away, some of her milk spills, and it produces the band of light known as the Milky Way.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-waller_hodge2003-49) In another Greek story, the abandoned Heracles is given by [Athena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Athena "Athena") to Hera for feeding, but Heracles' forcefulness causes Hera to rip him from her breast in pain.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-50)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Leeming-51)[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Pache-52)

In Western culture, the name "Milky Way" is derived from its appearance as a dim unresolved "milky" glowing band arching across the night sky. The term is a translation of the [Classical Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Latin "Classical Latin")_via lactea_, in turn derived from the [Hellenistic Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic_Greek "Hellenistic Greek")γαλαξίας, short for γαλαξίας κύκλος (_\_galaxías kýklos\__), meaning "milky circle". The [Ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek")γαλαξίας (_\_galaxias\__) – from root γαλακτ-, γάλα ("milk") + -ίας (forming adjectives) – is also the root of "galaxy", the name for our, and later all such, collections of stars.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-eo_galaxy-53)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-jankowski2010-54)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-oxford-55) The Milky Way, or "milk circle", was just one of 11 "circles" the Greeks identified in the sky, others being the [zodiac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zodiac "Zodiac"), the [meridian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy) "Meridian (astronomy)"), the [horizon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizon "Horizon"), the [equator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equator "Equator"), the [tropics of Cancer and Capricorn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropics_of_Cancer_and_Capricorn "Tropics of Cancer and Capricorn"), the [Arctic Circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Circle "Arctic Circle") and the [Antarctic Circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctic_Circle "Antarctic Circle"), and two [colure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colure "Colure") circles passing through both poles.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-eratosthenes1997-56) The English term can be traced back to a story by [Geoffrey Chaucer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer "Geoffrey Chaucer")c. 1380:

> See yonder, lo, the Galaxyë
> 
> Which men [clepeth](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clepe "wikt:clepe")_the Milky Wey_,
> 
> For hit is whyt: and somme, parfey,

### Common names

*   "Birds' Path" is used in several [Uralic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uralic_languages "Uralic languages") and [Turkic languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkic_languages "Turkic languages") and in the [Baltic languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_languages "Baltic languages"). Northern peoples observed that [migratory birds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Migratory_birds "Migratory birds") follow the course of the galaxy[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-58) while migrating at the Northern Hemisphere. The name "Birds' Path" (in Finnish, Estonian, Latvian, Lithuanian, Bashkir and Kazakh) has some variations in other languages, e.g. "Way of the grey (wild) goose" in Chuvash, Mari and Tatar and "Way of the Crane" in Erzya and Moksha.
*   House river: The [Kaurna people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaurna_people "Kaurna people") of the [Adelaide Plains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adelaide_Plains "Adelaide Plains") of South Australia called the Milky Way _wodliparri_ in the [Kaurna language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaurna_language "Kaurna language"), meaning "house river".[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-59)
*   Emu in the Sky: The [Gomeroi people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamilaraay "Gamilaraay") between [New South Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_South_Wales "New South Wales") and [Queensland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queensland "Queensland") called the Milky Way _Dhinawan_, the giant [Emu in the Sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emu "Emu") that it stretches across the night sky.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-60)
*   Milky Way: Many European languages have borrowed, directly or indirectly, the Greek name for the Milky Way, including English and Latin.
*   Road to Santiago: the Milky Way was traditionally used as a guide by [pilgrims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pilgrims "Pilgrims") traveling to the holy site at [Compostela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Santiago_de_Compostela "Santiago de Compostela"), hence the use of "The Road to Santiago" as a name for the Milky Way.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Fiona-61) Curiously, _La Voje Ladee_ "The Milky Way" was also used to refer to the pilgrimage road.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-62)
*   River Ganga of the Sky: this Sanskrit name (आकाशगंगा_Ākāśagaṃgā_) is used in many Indian languages following a Hindu belief .
*   Silver River: this Chinese name "Silver River" (銀河) is used throughout East Asia, including Korea and Vietnam (_Ngân hà_). In Japan and Korea, "Silver River" ([Japanese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language"): 銀河, [romanized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese "Romanization of Japanese"):_ginga_; [Korean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korean_language "Korean language"):은하; [RR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean "Revised Romanization of Korean"):_eunha_) means galaxies in general.
*   River of Heaven: The Japanese name for the Milky Way is the "River of Heaven" (天の川, _Amanokawa_), as well as an alternative name in Chinese ([Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_language "Chinese language"): 天河; [pinyin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinyin "Pinyin"): _Tiānhé_). In Vietnamese, "River of Heaven" (_Thiên hà_) means galaxies in general.
*   Straw Way: In West Asia, Central Asia and parts of the Balkans the name for the Milky Way is related to the word for [straw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Straw "Straw"). Today, Persians, Pakistanis, and Turks use it in addition to Arabs. It has been suggested that the term was spread by medieval [Arabs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab "Arab") who in turn borrowed it from Armenians.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-63)
*   Walsingham Way: In England the Milky Way was called the Walsingham Way in reference to the shrine of [Our Lady of Walsingham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Walsingham "Our Lady of Walsingham") which is in [Norfolk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norfolk "Norfolk"), England. It was understood to be either a guide to the pilgrims who flocked there, or a representation of the pilgrims themselves.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-64)
*   Winter Street: Scandinavian peoples, such as Swedes, have called the galaxy Winter Street (_Vintergatan_) as the galaxy is most clearly visible during the winter at the northern hemisphere, especially at high latitudes where the [glow of the Sun late at night](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midnight_sun "Midnight sun") can obscure it during the summer.

Appearance
----------

[![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Milky_Way_Night_Sky_Black_Rock_Desert_Nevada.jpg/250px-Milky_Way_Night_Sky_Black_Rock_Desert_Nevada.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Night_Sky_Black_Rock_Desert_Nevada.jpg)

The Milky Way as seen from a dark site with little [light pollution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_pollution "Light pollution")

The Milky Way is visible as a hazy band of white light, some 30° wide, arching in the [night sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Night_sky "Night sky").[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-pasachoff1994-65) Although all the individual naked-eye stars in the entire sky are part of the Milky Way Galaxy, the term "Milky Way" is limited to this band of light.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-66)[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-67) The light originates from the accumulation of [unresolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_resolution "Angular resolution") stars and other material located in the direction of the [galactic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_plane "Galactic plane"). Brighter regions around the band appear as soft visual patches known as [star clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_cloud "Star cloud"). The most conspicuous of these is the [Large Sagittarius Star Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_Sagittarius_Star_Cloud "Large Sagittarius Star Cloud"), a portion of the central [bulge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_bulge "Galactic bulge") of the galaxy.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-68) Dark regions within the band, such as the [Great Rift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Rift_(astronomy) "Great Rift (astronomy)") and the [Coalsack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coalsack_Nebula "Coalsack Nebula"), are areas where [interstellar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_dust "Interstellar dust") blocks light from distant stars. Peoples of the southern hemisphere, including the [Inca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coricancha#Inca_astronomy "Coricancha") and [Australian Aborigines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Aboriginal_astronomy#Emu_in_the_sky "Australian Aboriginal astronomy"), identified these regions as [dark cloud constellations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_cloud_constellation "Dark cloud constellation").[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-69) The area of sky that the Milky Way obscures is called the [Zone of Avoidance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zone_of_Avoidance "Zone of Avoidance").[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-70)

The Milky Way has a relatively low [surface brightness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_brightness "Surface brightness"). Its visibility can be greatly reduced by background light, such as [light pollution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_pollution "Light pollution") or moonlight. The sky needs to be darker than about 20.2 [magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy) "Magnitude (astronomy)") per square arcsecond in order for the Milky Way to be visible.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-71) It should be visible if the [limiting magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limiting_magnitude "Limiting magnitude") is approximately +5.1 or better and shows a great deal of detail at +6.1.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-steinicke_sakiel2007-72) This makes the Milky Way difficult to see from brightly lit urban or suburban areas, but very prominent when viewed from [rural areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rural_area "Rural area") when the Moon is below the horizon.[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-bortle-73) Maps of artificial night sky brightness show that more than one-third of Earth's population cannot see the Milky Way from their homes due to light pollution.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-74)

As viewed from Earth, the visible region of the Milky Way's [galactic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_plane "Galactic plane") occupies an area of the sky that includes 30 [constellations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation "Constellation").[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-const-75) The [Galactic Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center") lies in the direction of [Sagittarius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation) "Sagittarius (constellation)"), where the Milky Way is brightest. From Sagittarius, the hazy band of white light appears to pass around to the [galactic anticenter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_anticenter "Galactic anticenter") in [Auriga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auriga_(constellation) "Auriga (constellation)"). The band then continues the rest of the way around the sky, back to Sagittarius, dividing the sky into two roughly equal [hemispheres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_sphere "Celestial sphere").[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-76)

The galactic plane is inclined by about 60° to the [ecliptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic "Ecliptic") (the path of the Sun in the sky). It is tilted at an angle of 63° to the [celestial equator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_equator "Celestial equator").[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-77)

Astronomical history
--------------------

### Ancient, naked eye observations

In _[Meteorologica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology\_(Aristotle) "Meteorology (Aristotle)")_, [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle") (384–322 BC) states that the [Greek philosophers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_philosophy "Greek philosophy")[Anaxagoras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaxagoras "Anaxagoras") (c. 500–428 BC) and [Democritus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democritus "Democritus") (460–370 BC) proposed that the Milky Way is the glow of stars not directly visible due to Earth's shadow, while other stars receive their light from the Sun, but have their glow obscured by solar rays.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-78) Aristotle himself believed that the Milky Way was part of the Earth's upper atmosphere, along with the stars, and that it was a byproduct of stars burning that did not dissipate because of its outermost location in the atmosphere, composing its [great circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_circle "Great circle"). He said that the milky appearance of the Milky Way [Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy") is due to the refraction of the Earth's atmosphere.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-What_does_your_image_show-79)[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Montada-80)[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-81) The [Neoplatonist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoplatonism "Neoplatonism") philosopher [Olympiodorus the Younger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger "Olympiodorus the Younger") (c. 495–570 AD) criticized this view, arguing that if the Milky Way were [sublunary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sublunary "Sublunary"), it should appear different at different times and places on Earth, and that it should have [parallax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallax "Parallax"), which it does not. In his view, the Milky Way is celestial. This idea would be influential later in the [Muslim world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_world "Muslim world").[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-heidarzadeh2008-82)

The [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_people "Persian people") astronomer [Al-Biruni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Biruni "Al-Biruni") (973–1048) proposed that the Milky Way is "a collection of countless fragments of the nature of [nebulous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebula "Nebula") stars".[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mactutor_albirundi-83) The [Andalusian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Andalus "Al-Andalus") astronomer [Avempace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avempace "Avempace") (died 1138) proposed that the Milky Way was made up of many stars but appeared to be a continuous image in the Earth's atmosphere, citing his observation of a [conjunction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology) "Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)") of Jupiter and Mars in 1106 or 1107 as evidence.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Montada-80) The Persian astronomer [Nasir al-Din al-Tusi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi "Nasir al-Din al-Tusi") (1201–1274) in his _Tadhkira_ wrote: "The Milky Way, i.e. the Galaxy, is made up of a very large number of small, tightly clustered stars, which, on account of their concentration and smallness, seem to be cloudy patches. Because of this, it was likened to milk in color."[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ragep1993-84)[Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Qayyim_al-Jawziyya "Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya") (1292–1350) proposed that the Milky Way is "a myriad of tiny stars packed together in the sphere of the fixed stars".[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Livingston-85)

### Telescopic observations

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/250px-Herschel-Galaxy.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Herschel-Galaxy.png)

The shape of the Milky Way as deduced from star counts by [William Herschel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Herschel "William Herschel") in 1785. The [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") was assumed to be near the center

Proof of the Milky Way consisting of many stars came in 1610 when [Galileo Galilei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") used a [telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_telescope "Optical telescope") to study the Milky Way and discovered that it was composed of a huge number of faint stars. Galileo also concluded that the appearance of the Milky Way was due to [refraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refraction "Refraction") of the Earth's atmosphere.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-86)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-oconnor_robertson2002-87)[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-What_does_your_image_show-79) In a treatise in 1755, [Immanuel Kant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immanuel_Kant "Immanuel Kant"), drawing on earlier work by [Thomas Wright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Wright_(astronomer) "Thomas Wright (astronomer)"),[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-88) speculated (correctly) that the Milky Way might be a rotating body of a huge number of stars, held together by [gravitational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitation "Gravitation") forces akin to the Solar System but on much larger scales.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-89) The resulting disk of stars would be seen as a band in the sky from our perspective inside the disk. Wright and Kant also conjectured that some of the [nebulae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebula "Nebula") visible in the night sky might be separate "galaxies" themselves, similar to our own. Kant referred to both the Milky Way and the "extragalactic nebulae" as "island universes", a term still current up to the 1930s.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-90)[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-our_galaxy-91)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-92)

The first attempt to describe the shape of the Milky Way and the position of the Sun within it was carried out by [William Herschel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Herschel "William Herschel") in 1785 by carefully counting the number of stars in different regions of the visible sky. He produced a diagram of the shape of the Milky Way with the Solar System close to the center.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-93)

In 1845, [Lord Rosse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Parsons,_3rd_Earl_of_Rosse "William Parsons, 3rd Earl of Rosse") constructed a new telescope and was able to distinguish between elliptical and spiral-shaped nebulae. He also managed to make out individual point sources in some of these nebulae, lending credence to Kant's earlier conjecture.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-abbey-94)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-95)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/250px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pic_iroberts1.jpg)

Photograph of the "Great Andromeda Nebula" from 1899, later identified as the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy")

In 1904, studying the [proper motions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proper_motion "Proper motion") of stars, [Jacobus Kapteyn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobus_Kapteyn "Jacobus Kapteyn") reported that these were not random, as it was believed in that time; stars could be divided into two streams, moving in nearly opposite directions.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-96) It was later realized that Kapteyn's data had been the first evidence of the rotation of the Milky Way,[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-97) which ultimately led to the finding of galactic rotation by [Bertil Lindblad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertil_Lindblad "Bertil Lindblad") and [Jan Oort](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jan_Oort "Jan Oort").

In 1917, [Heber Doust Curtis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heber_Doust_Curtis "Heber Doust Curtis") had observed the nova [S Andromedae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S_Andromedae "S Andromedae") within the [Great Andromeda Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Andromeda_Nebula "Great Andromeda Nebula") ([Messier object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messier_object "Messier object") 31). Searching the photographic record, he found 11 more [novae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nova "Nova"). Curtis noticed that these novae were, on average, 10 [magnitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy) "Magnitude (astronomy)") fainter than those that occurred within the Milky Way. As a result, he was able to come up with a distance estimate of 150,000 parsecs. He became a proponent of the "island universes" hypothesis, which held that the spiral nebulae were independent galaxies.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-98)[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-pasp100_6-99) In 1920 the [Great Debate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy) "Great Debate (astronomy)") took place between [Harlow Shapley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harlow_Shapley "Harlow Shapley") and Heber Curtis, concerning the nature of the Milky Way, spiral nebulae, and the dimensions of the Universe. To support his claim that the Great Andromeda Nebula is an external galaxy, Curtis noted the appearance of dark lanes resembling the dust clouds in the Milky Way, as well as the significant [Doppler shift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_effect "Doppler effect").[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-weaver-100)

The controversy was conclusively settled by [Edwin Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_Hubble "Edwin Hubble") in the early 1920s using the Mount Wilson observatory [2.5 m (100 in) Hooker telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hooker_telescope "Hooker telescope"). With the [light-gathering power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-gathering_power "Light-gathering power") of this new telescope, he was able to produce [astronomical photographs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrophotography "Astrophotography") that resolved the outer parts of some spiral nebulae as collections of individual stars. He was also able to identify some [Cepheid variables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cepheid_variable "Cepheid variable") that he could use as a [benchmark](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/benchmark "wikt:benchmark") to estimate the distance to the nebulae. He found that the Andromeda Nebula is 275,000 parsecs from the Sun, far too distant to be part of the Milky Way.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-jrasc83_6-101)[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj69_103-102)

### Satellite observations

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Galaxymap.com%2C_map_5000_parsecs_%282022%29.png/330px-Galaxymap.com%2C_map_5000_parsecs_%282022%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Galaxymap.com,_map_5000_parsecs_(2022).png)

Map of stars cataloged by the Gaia release in 2021, displayed as density mesh in the diagram

The [ESA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") spacecraft _[Gaia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia\_(spacecraft) "Gaia (spacecraft)")_ provides distance estimates by determining the [parallax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallax "Parallax") of a billion stars and is mapping the Milky Way.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-103)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-104)

Data from _Gaia_ has been described as "transformational". It has been estimated that _Gaia_ has expanded the number of observations of stars from about 2 million stars, as of the 1990s, to 2 billion. It has expanded the measurable volume of space by a factor of 100 in radius and a factor of 1,000 in precision.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Skibba-105)

A study in 2020 concluded that _Gaia_ detected a wobbling motion of the galaxy, which might be caused by "[torques](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torques "Torques") from a misalignment of the disc's rotation axis with respect to the principal axis of a non-spherical halo, or from [accreted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics) "Accretion (astrophysics)") matter in the halo acquired during late infall, or from nearby, interacting satellite galaxies and their consequent tides".[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-106) In April 2024, initial studies and related maps, involving the [magnetic fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field") of the Milky Way were reported.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NYT-20240419-107)

Astrography
-----------

### Sun's location and neighborhood

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Galaxymap.com%2C_map_12000_parsecs_%282022%29.png/330px-Galaxymap.com%2C_map_12000_parsecs_%282022%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Galaxymap.com,_map_12000_parsecs_(2022).png)

Map of stars cataloged by the _Gaia_ release in 2021, overlay on top of artist's conception of the Milky Way overall shape

The [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") is near the inner rim of the [Orion Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Arm "Orion Arm"), within the [Local Fluff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Fluff "Local Fluff") of the [Local Bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Bubble "Local Bubble"), between the [Radcliffe wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radcliffe_wave "Radcliffe wave") and _Split_ linear structures (formerly [Gould Belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gould_Belt "Gould Belt")).[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Alves_Zucker_Goodman_Speagle_2020-108) Based upon studies of stellar orbits around Sgr A* by Gillessen _et al._ (2016), the Sun lies at an estimated distance of 27.14±0.46 kly (8.32±0.14 kpc)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Gillessen2016-39) from the Galactic Center. Boehle _et al._ (2016) found a smaller value of 25.64±0.46 kly (7.86±0.14 kpc), also using a star orbit analysis.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-boehle2016-109) The Sun is currently 5–30 parsecs (16–98 ly) above, or north of, the central plane of the Galactic disk.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess2009-110) The distance between the local arm and the next arm out, the [Perseus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perseus_Arm "Perseus Arm"), is about 2,000 parsecs (6,500 ly).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-fn9-111) The Sun, and thus the Solar System, is located in the Milky Way's [galactic habitable zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_habitable_zone "Galactic habitable zone").[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mullen-2001-112)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-sundin-2006-113)

There are about 208 stars brighter than [absolute magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_magnitude "Absolute magnitude")8.5 within a sphere with a radius of 15 parsecs (49 ly) from the Sun, giving a density of one star per 69 cubic parsecs, or one star per 2,360 cubic light-years (from [List of nearest bright stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_nearest_bright_stars "List of nearest bright stars")). On the other hand, there are 64 known stars (of any magnitude, not counting 4[brown dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_dwarf "Brown dwarf")) within 5 parsecs (16 ly) of the Sun, giving a density of about one star per 8.2 cubic parsecs, or one per 284 cubic light-years (from [List of nearest stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars "List of nearest stars")). This illustrates the fact that there are far more faint stars than bright stars: in the entire sky, there are about 500 stars brighter than [apparent magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent magnitude")4 but 15.5 million stars brighter than apparent magnitude 14.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-114)

The apex of the Sun's way, or the [solar apex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_apex "Solar apex"), is the direction that the Sun travels through the [Local standard of rest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_standard_of_rest "Local standard of rest") in the Milky Way. The general direction of the Sun's Galactic motion is towards the star [Deneb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deneb "Deneb") near the constellation of [Cygnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation) "Cygnus (constellation)"), at an angle of roughly 90 sky degrees to the direction of the Galactic Center. The Sun's orbit about the Milky Way is expected to be roughly elliptical with the addition of perturbations due to the Galactic spiral arms and non-uniform mass distributions. In addition, the Sun passes through the Galactic plane approximately 2.7 times per orbit.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-115) This is very similar to how a [simple harmonic oscillator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_harmonic_oscillator "Simple harmonic oscillator") works with no drag force (damping) term. These oscillations were until recently thought to coincide with [mass lifeform extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_extinction "Mass extinction") periods on Earth.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-extinction-116) A reanalysis of the effects of the Sun's transit through the spiral structure based on CO data has failed to find a correlation.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-overholt_etal-117)

It takes the Solar System about 240 million years to complete one orbit of the Milky Way (a [galactic year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_year "Galactic year")),[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-sparke_gallagher-118) so the Sun is thought to have completed 18–20 orbits during its lifetime and 1/1250 of a revolution since the [origin of humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_humans "Origin of humans"). The [orbital speed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_speed "Orbital speed") of the Solar System about the center of the Milky Way is approximately 220 km/s (490,000 mph) or 0.073% of the [speed of light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light "Speed of light"). The Sun moves through the heliosphere at 84,000 km/h (52,000 mph). At this speed, it takes around 1,400 years for the Solar System to travel a distance of 1 light-year, or 8 days to travel 1 AU ([astronomical unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit "Astronomical unit")).[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-garlick2002-119) The Solar System is headed in the direction of the zodiacal constellation [Scorpius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scorpius "Scorpius"), which follows the ecliptic.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-120)

### Galactic quadrants

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Galactic_longitude.JPG/250px-Galactic_longitude.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Galactic_longitude.JPG)

A diagram of the Sun's location in the Milky Way; the angles represent longitudes in the [galactic coordinate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_coordinate_system "Galactic coordinate system")

A galactic quadrant, or quadrant of the Milky Way, refers to one of four circular sectors in the division of the Milky Way. In astronomical practice, the delineation of the galactic quadrants is based upon the [galactic coordinate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_coordinate_system "Galactic coordinate system"), which places the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") as the [origin of the mapping system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system "Polar coordinate system").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-121)

Quadrants are described using [ordinals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinal_number_(linguistics) "Ordinal number (linguistics)")– for example, "1st galactic quadrant",[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-toolsofradio-122) "second galactic quadrant",[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NASAAD131K-123) or "third quadrant of the Milky Way".[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lampton1997-124) Viewing from the [north galactic pole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_galactic_pole "North galactic pole") with 0° [(zero degrees)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_(angle) "Degree (angle)") as the [ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_(geometry) "Ray (geometry)") that runs starting from the Sun and through the Galactic Center, the quadrants are:

| Galactic quadrant | Galactic longitude [(ℓ)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_longitude "Galactic longitude") | Reference |
| --- | --- | --- |
| 1st | 0° ≤ ℓ ≤ 90° | [[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-vanWoerden-Strom-2006-125) |
| 2nd | 90° ≤ ℓ ≤ 180° | [[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NASAAD131K-123) |
| 3rd | 180° ≤ ℓ ≤ 270° | [[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lampton1997-124) |
| 4th | 270° ≤ ℓ ≤ 360° (360° ≅ 0°) | [[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-toolsofradio-122) |

with the galactic longitude [(ℓ)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_longitude "Galactic longitude") increasing in the counter-clockwise direction ([positive rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle#Positive_and_negative_angles "Angle")) as viewed from [north](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_north "Galactic north") of the [Galactic Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center") (a view-point several hundred thousand [light-years](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year "Light-year") distant from Earth in the direction of the constellation [Coma Berenices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coma_Berenices "Coma Berenices")); if viewed from south of the Galactic Center (a view-point similarly distant in the constellation [Sculptor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sculptor_(constellation) "Sculptor (constellation)")), [ℓ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_longitude "Galactic longitude") would increase in the clockwise direction ([negative rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle#Positive_and_negative_angles "Angle")).

Size and mass
-------------

### Size

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Local_Group_Galaxies_Comparison.png/330px-Local_Group_Galaxies_Comparison.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Local_Group_Galaxies_Comparison.png)

A size comparison of the six largest galaxies of the [Local Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Group "Local Group"), including the Milky Way

The Milky Way is one of the two largest galaxies in the [Local Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Group "Local Group") (the other being the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy")), although the size for its [galactic disc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_disc "Galactic disc") and how much it defines the isophotal diameter is not well understood.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lopez2018-12) It is estimated that the significant bulk of stars in the galaxy lies within the 26 kiloparsecs (80,000 light-years) diameter, and that the number of stars beyond the outermost disc dramatically reduces to a very low number, with respect to an extrapolation of the exponential disk with the scale length of the inner disc.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-arxiv0909.3857-126)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lopez2018-12)

There are several methods being used in astronomy in defining the size of a galaxy, and each of them can yield different results with respect to one another. The most commonly employed method is the [D 25 standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy#Isophotal_diameter "Galaxy") – the [isophote](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isophote "Isophote") where the photometric brightness of a galaxy in the B-band (445 nm wavelength of light, in the blue part of the [visible spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum")) reaches 25 mag/arcsec 2.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Dimensions-127) An estimate from 1997 by Goodwin and others compared the distribution of [Cepheid variable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cepheid_variable "Cepheid variable") stars in 17 other spiral galaxies to the ones in the Milky Way, and modelling the relationship to their surface brightnesses. This gave an [isophotal diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isophotal_diameter "Isophotal diameter") for the Milky Way at 26.8±1.1 kiloparsecs (87,400±3,600 light-years), by assuming that the galactic disc is well represented by an exponential disc and adopting a central surface brightness of the galaxy (μ 0) of 22.1±0.3 _B_-mag/arcsec−2 and a disk scale length (_h_) of 5.0±0.5 kpc (16,300±1,600 ly).[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Average-128)[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Goodwin-11)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Castro-129)

This is significantly smaller than the Andromeda Galaxy's isophotal diameter, and slightly below the mean isophotal sizes of the galaxies being at 28.3 kpc (92,000 ly).[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Goodwin-11) The paper concludes that the Milky Way and Andromeda Galaxy were not overly large spiral galaxies, nor were among the [largest known](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_galaxies "List of largest galaxies") (if the former not being the largest) as previously widely believed, but rather average ordinary spiral galaxies.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Goodwin_Hubble-130) To compare the relative physical scale of the Milky Way, if the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") out to [Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune "Neptune") were the size of a [US quarter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin) "Quarter (United States coin)") (24.3 mm (0.955 in)), the Milky Way would be approximately at least the greatest north–south line of the [contiguous United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contiguous_United_States "Contiguous United States").[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-131) An even older study from 1978 gave a lower diameter for Milky Way of about 23 kpc (75,000 ly).[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Goodwin-11)

A 2015 paper discovered that there is a ring-like filament of stars called Triangulum–Andromeda Ring (TriAnd Ring) rippling above and below the relatively flat [galactic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_plane "Galactic plane"), which alongside [Monoceros Ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monoceros_Ring "Monoceros Ring") were both suggested to be primarily the result of disk oscillations and wrapping around the Milky Way, at a diameter of at least 50 kpc (160,000 ly),[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-132) which may be part of the Milky Way's outer disk itself, hence making the stellar disk larger by increasing to this size.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-rpi2015-133) A more recent 2018 paper later somewhat ruled out this hypothesis, and supported a conclusion that the Monoceros Ring, [A13](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=A13_(stellar_overdensity)&action=edit&redlink=1 "A13 (stellar overdensity) (page does not exist)") and TriAnd Ring were stellar overdensities rather kicked out from the main stellar disk, with the velocity dispersion of the RR Lyrae stars found to be higher and consistent with halo membership.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-allyson-134)

Another 2018 study revealed the very probable presence of disk stars at 26–31.5 kpc (84,800–103,000 ly) from the Galactic Center or perhaps even farther, significantly beyond approximately 13–20 kpc (40,000–70,000 ly), in which it was once believed to be the abrupt drop-off of the stellar density of the disk, meaning that few or no stars were expected to be above this limit, save for stars that belong to the old population of the galactic halo.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Lopez2018-12)[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nbcnews1-135)[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-136)

A 2020 study predicted the edge of the Milky Way's [dark matter halo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter_halo "Dark matter halo") being around 292±61[kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs "Parsec") (952,000±199,000[ly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year "Light-year")), which translates to a diameter of 584±122[kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs "Parsec") (1.905±0.3979[Mly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year#Definitions "Light-year")).[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-croswell2020-31)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-dearson2020-32) The Milky Way's stellar disk is also estimated to be approximately up to 1.35 kpc (4,000 ly) thick.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ask-astro-137)[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Rix_Bovy-138)

### Mass

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milky_way_profile.svg/330px-Milky_way_profile.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Milky_way_profile.svg)

A schematic profile of the Milky Way.

Abbreviations: GNP/GSP: Galactic North and South Poles

The Milky Way is approximately 0.88 trillion times the mass of the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") in total (0.88×10 12 solar masses), using a cutoff of 200kpc to define the galaxy.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-139) Estimates of the mass of the Milky Way vary, depending upon the method and data used. The low end of the estimate range is 5.8×10 11[solar masses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass") (M☉), somewhat less than that of the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy").[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Karachentsevetal2006-140)[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mass-141)[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-galmass-142)

Measurements using the [Very Long Baseline Array](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Very_Long_Baseline_Array "Very Long Baseline Array") in 2009 found velocities as large as 254 km/s (570,000 mph) for stars at the outer edge of the Milky Way.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nrao20090105-143) Because the orbital velocity depends on the total mass inside the orbital radius, this suggests that the Milky Way is more massive, roughly equaling the mass of Andromeda Galaxy at 7×10 11 M☉ within 160,000 ly (49 kpc) of its center.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-reid2009-144) In 2010, a measurement of the radial velocity of halo stars found that the mass enclosed within 80 kilo[parsecs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec "Parsec") is 7×10 11 M☉.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-gnedin2010-145) In a 2014 study, the mass of the entire Milky Way is estimated to be 8.5×10 11 M☉,[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Jorge_Pe%C3%B1arrubia2014-146) but this is only half the mass of the Andromeda Galaxy.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Jorge_Pe%C3%B1arrubia2014-146) A recent 2019 mass estimate for the Milky Way is 1.29×10 12 M☉.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-147)

Much of the mass of the Milky Way seems to be [dark matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter "Dark matter"), an unknown and invisible form of matter that interacts gravitationally with ordinary matter. A [dark matter halo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter_halo "Dark matter halo") is conjectured to spread out relatively uniformly to a distance beyond one hundred kiloparsecs (kpc) from the Galactic Center. Mathematical models of the Milky Way suggest that the mass of dark matter is 1–1.5×10 12 M☉.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-McMillan2011-148)[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-McMillan2016-149)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-150) 2013 and 2014 studies indicate a range in mass, as large as 4.5×10 12 M☉[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-151) and as small as 8×10 11 M☉.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-152) By comparison, the total mass of all the stars in the Milky Way is estimated to be between 4.6×10 10 M☉[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Licquia2013-153) and 6.43×10 10 M☉.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-McMillan2011-148)

In addition to the stars, there is also interstellar gas, comprising 90% [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") and 10% [helium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium "Helium") by mass,[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ism1-154) with two thirds of the hydrogen found in the [atomic form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_hydrogen "Neutral hydrogen") and the remaining one-third as [molecular hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen "Molecular hydrogen").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ism2-155) The mass of the Milky Way's interstellar gas is equal to between 10%[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ism2-155) and 15%[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ism1-154) of the total mass of its stars. [Interstellar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_dust "Interstellar dust") accounts for an additional 1% of the total mass of the gas.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ism1-154)

In March 2019, astronomers reported that the [virial mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virial_mass "Virial mass") of the Milky Way Galaxy is 1.54×10 12[solar masses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_masses "Solar masses") within a [radius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radius "Radius") of about 39.5 kpc (130,000 ly), over twice as much as was determined in earlier studies, suggesting that about 90% of the mass of the galaxy is [dark matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter "Dark matter").[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-SA-20190308-8)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ARX-20190208-9)

In September 2023, astronomers reported that the [virial mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virial_mass "Virial mass") of the Milky Way Galaxy is only 2.06×10 11[solar masses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_masses "Solar masses"), only a tenth of the mass of previous studies. The mass was determined from data of the [_Gaia_ spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia_(spacecraft) "Gaia (spacecraft)").[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-aA-20230927-156)

Contents
--------

The Milky Way contains between 100 and 400 billion stars[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-frommert_kronberg2005-14)[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-wethington2010-15) and at least that many planets.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-villard20120111-157) An exact figure would depend on counting the number of very-low-mass stars, which are difficult to detect, especially at distances of more than 300 ly (90 pc) from the Sun. As a comparison, the neighboring Andromeda Galaxy contains an estimated one trillion (10 12) stars.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-young2006-158) The Milky Way may contain ten billion [white dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_dwarf "White dwarf"), a billion [neutron stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_star "Neutron star"), and a hundred million stellar [black holes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_hole "Black hole").[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-number-161)[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-162)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-163) Filling the space between the stars is a disk of gas and dust called the [interstellar medium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_medium "Interstellar medium"). This disk has at least a comparable extent in radius to the stars,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-science312_1773-164) whereas the thickness of the gas layer ranges from hundreds of light-years for the colder gas to thousands of light-years for the warmer gas.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-araa28_215-165)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj702_1472-166)

The disk of stars in the Milky Way does not have a sharp edge beyond which there are no stars. Rather, the concentration of stars decreases with distance from the center of the Milky Way. Beyond a radius of roughly 40,000 light years (13 kpc) from the center, the number of stars per cubic [parsec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec "Parsec") drops much faster with radius.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-arxiv0909.3857-126) Surrounding the galactic disk is a spherical [galactic halo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_halo "Galactic halo") of stars and [globular clusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globular_cluster "Globular cluster") that extends farther outward, but is limited in size by the orbits of two Milky Way satellites, the Large and Small [Magellanic Clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds "Magellanic Clouds"), whose [closest approach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apsis "Apsis") to the Galactic Center is about 180,000 ly (55 kpc).[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mnras371_1_108-167) At this distance or beyond, the orbits of most halo objects would be disrupted by the Magellanic Clouds. Hence, such objects would probably be ejected from the vicinity of the Milky Way. The integrated [absolute visual magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_visual_magnitude "Absolute visual magnitude") of the Milky Way is estimated to be around −20.9.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-coffey-168)[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Karachentsev2004-169)[[g]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-milky_way_mag-170)

Both [gravitational microlensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing "Gravitational microlensing") and planetary transit observations indicate that there may be at least as many planets bound to stars as there are stars in the Milky Way,[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Nature-20120111-37)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-borenstein2011-171) and microlensing measurements indicate that there are more [rogue planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rogue_planet "Rogue planet") not bound to host stars than there are stars.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-172)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nasa20110526-173) The Milky Way contains an average of at least one planet per star, resulting in 100–400 billion planets, according to a January 2013 study of the five-planet star system [Kepler-32](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler-32 "Kepler-32") by the [Kepler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft) "Kepler (spacecraft)") space observatory.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Space-20130102-38) A different January 2013 analysis of Kepler data estimated that at least 17 billion [Earth-sized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet")[exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet "Exoplanet") reside in the Milky Way.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Space-Billions-174)

In November 2013, astronomers reported, based on [_Kepler_ space mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft) "Kepler (spacecraft)") data, that there could be as many as 40 billion Earth-sized [planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extrasolar_planet "Extrasolar planet") orbiting in the [habitable zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitable_zone "Habitable zone") of [Sun-like stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_analog "Solar analog") and [red dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf") within the Milky Way.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NYT-20131104-175)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-176)[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nov2013_planet_estimate-177) 11 billion of these estimated planets may be orbiting Sun-like stars.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-LATimes-20131104-178) The nearest exoplanet may be 4.2 light-years away, orbiting the [red dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf")[Proxima Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxima_Centauri "Proxima Centauri"), according to a 2016 study.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Planet-Alpha-Centauri-179) Such Earth-sized planets may be more numerous than gas giants,[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Nature-20120111-37) though harder to detect at great distances given their small size. Besides exoplanets, "[exocomets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exocomet "Exocomet")", [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet") beyond the Solar System, have also been detected and may be common in the Milky Way.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Space-Exocomets-180) More recently, in November 2020, over 300 million habitable exoplanets are estimated to exist in the Milky Way Galaxy.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NYT-20201105-181)

When compared to other more distant galaxies in the universe, the Milky Way galaxy has a below average amount of [neutrino](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrino "Neutrino") luminosity making our galaxy a "neutrino desert".[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-182)

Structure
---------

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Milky_Way_side_view.png/500px-Milky_Way_side_view.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Milky_Way_side_view.png)

Overview of different elements of the overall structure of the Milky Way

The Milky Way consists of a bar-shaped core region surrounded by a warped disk of [gas, dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_medium "Interstellar medium") and stars.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-183)[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-184) The mass distribution within the Milky Way closely resembles the type Sbc in the [Hubble classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_sequence "Hubble sequence"), which represents spiral galaxies with relatively loosely wound arms.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ssr100_1_129-6) Astronomers first began to conjecture that the Milky Way is a [barred spiral galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy "Barred spiral galaxy"), rather than an ordinary [spiral galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral_galaxy "Spiral galaxy"), in the 1960s.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-185)[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-186)[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-187) These conjectures were confirmed by the [Spitzer Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope "Spitzer Space Telescope") observations in 2005 that showed the Milky Way's central bar to be larger than previously thought.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-fn3-188)

### Galactic Center

[![Image 11: A dark spot surrounded by doughnut shaped orange-yellow ring](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/EHT_Saggitarius_A_black_hole.tif/lossy-page1-192px-EHT_Saggitarius_A_black_hole.tif.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:EHT_Saggitarius_A_black_hole.tif)

The Sun is 25,000–28,000 ly (7.7–8.6 kpc) from the Galactic Center. This value is estimated using [geometric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometric "Geometric")-based methods or by measuring selected astronomical objects that serve as [standard candles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_candles "Standard candles"), with different techniques yielding various values within this approximate range.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj692_2_1075-190)[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-boehle2016-109)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Gillessen2016-39)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ReideaSgrb2009-191)[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-vanhollebeke09-192)[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess10-193) In the inner few kiloparsecs (around 10,000 light-years radius) is a dense concentration of mostly old stars in a roughly spheroidal shape called [the bulge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy) "Bulge (astronomy)").[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Galactic_bulge-194) It has been proposed that the Milky Way lacks a [bulge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_bulge#Classical_bulges "Galactic bulge") due to a [collision and merger between previous galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_merger "Galactic merger"), and that instead it only has a [_pseudobulge_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_bulge#Disk-like_Bulges "Galactic bulge") formed by its central bar.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apjl_1_l72-195) However, confusion in the literature between the (peanut shell)-shaped structure created by instabilities in the bar, versus a possible bulge with an expected half-light radius of 0.5 kpc, abounds.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-196)

The Galactic Center is marked by an intense [radio source](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_astronomy "Radio astronomy") named [Sagittarius A*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_A* "Sagittarius A*") (pronounced _Sagittarius A-star_). The motion of material around the center indicates that Sagittarius A* harbors a massive, compact object.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Jones2004-197) This concentration of mass is best explained as a [supermassive black hole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole "Supermassive black hole")[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-chandra2003-198)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj692_2_1075-190)[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj689_2_1044-199) (SMBH) with an estimated mass of 4.1–4.5 million times the [mass of the Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass").[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj689_2_1044-199) The rate of accretion of the SMBH is consistent with an [inactive galactic nucleus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus "Active galactic nucleus"), being estimated at 1×10−5 M☉ per year.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-wang13-200) Observations indicate that there are SMBHs located near the center of most normal galaxies.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-blandford1999-201)[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-frolov_zelnikov2011-202)

The nature of the Milky Way's bar is actively debated, with estimates for its half-length and orientation spanning from 1 to 5 kpc (3,000–16,000 ly) and 10–50 degrees relative to the line of sight from Earth to the Galactic Center.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-vanhollebeke09-192)[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess10-193)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Cabrera-Lavers08-203) Certain authors advocate that the Milky Way features two distinct bars, one nestled within the other.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nishiyama06-204) However, [RR Lyrae-type](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RR_Lyrae_variable "RR Lyrae variable") stars do not trace a prominent Galactic bar.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess10-193)[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-alcock98-205)[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-kunder08-206) The bar may be surrounded by a ring called the "5 kpc ring" that contains a large fraction of the molecular hydrogen present in the Milky Way, as well as most of the Milky Way's [star formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_formation "Star formation") activity. Viewed from the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy"), it would be the brightest feature of the Milky Way.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-fn14-207) X-ray emission from the core is aligned with the massive stars surrounding the central bar[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-wang13-200) and the [Galactic ridge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_ridge "Galactic ridge").[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-nature318_267-208)

In June 2023, astronomers led by [Naoko Kurahashi Neilson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naoko_Kurahashi_Neilson "Naoko Kurahashi Neilson") reported using a new cascade neutrino technique[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-209) to detect, for the first time, the release of [neutrinos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrino "Neutrino") from the [galactic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_plane "Galactic plane") of the Milky Way [galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy"), creating the first neutrino view of the Milky Way.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NYT-20230629-210)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-SCI-20230629-211)

#### Gamma rays and x-rays

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/SRG-eROSITA_all-sky_image.jpg/500px-SRG-eROSITA_all-sky_image.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SRG-eROSITA_all-sky_image.jpg)

All-sky x-ray image

Since 1970, various gamma-ray detection missions have discovered 511-[keV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KeV "KeV")[gamma rays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma_rays "Gamma rays") coming from the general direction of the Galactic Center. These gamma rays are produced by [positrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positrons "Positrons") (antielectrons) annihilating with [electrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron "Electron"). In 2008 it was found that the distribution of the sources of the gamma rays resembles the distribution of low-mass [X-ray binaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray_binaries "X-ray binaries"), seeming to indicate that these X-ray binaries are sending positrons (and electrons) into interstellar space where they slow down and annihilate.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-212)[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NASA-2008-213)[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-214) The observations were made by both [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") and [ESA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency")'s satellites. In 1970 gamma ray detectors found that the emitting region was about 10,000 light-years across with a luminosity of about 10,000 Suns.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NASA-2008-213)

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg/500px-800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:800_nasa_structure_renderin2.jpg)

Illustration of the two gigantic [X-ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray "X-ray")/[gamma-ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma-ray "Gamma-ray") bubbles (blue-violet) of the Milky Way (center)

In 2010, two gigantic spherical bubbles of high energy gamma-emission were detected to the north and the south of the Milky Way core, using data from the [Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope "Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope"). The diameter of each of the bubbles is about 25,000 light-years (7.7 kpc) (or about 1/4 of the galaxy's estimated diameter); they stretch up to [Grus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grus_(constellation) "Grus (constellation)") and to [Virgo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_(constellation) "Virgo (constellation)") on the night-sky of the southern hemisphere.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-overbye2010-215)[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-sz2010-216) Subsequently, observations with the [Parkes Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parkes_Telescope "Parkes Telescope") at radio frequencies identified polarized emission that is associated with the Fermi bubbles. These observations are best interpreted as a magnetized outflow driven by star formation in the central 640 ly (200 pc) of the Milky Way.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-217)

Later, on January 5, 2015, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") reported observing an [X-ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray "X-ray") flare 400 times brighter than usual, a record-breaker, from Sagittarius A*. The unusual event may have been caused by the breaking apart of an [asteroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid "Asteroid") falling into the black hole or by the entanglement of [magnetic field lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field_lines "Magnetic field lines") within gas flowing into Sagittarius A*.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-NASA-20150105-189)

### Spiral arms

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Milky_Way_Arms.svg/500px-Milky_Way_Arms.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arms.svg)

Observed (normal lines) and extrapolated (dotted lines) structure of the spiral arms of the Milky Way, viewed from north of the galaxy – the galaxy rotates clockwise in this view. The gray lines radiating from the Sun's position (upper center) list the three-letter abbreviations of the corresponding constellations

Outside the gravitational influence of the Galactic bar, the structure of the interstellar medium and stars in the disk of the Milky Way is organized into four spiral arms.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-churchwell09-218) Spiral arms typically contain a higher density of interstellar gas and dust than the Galactic average as well as a greater concentration of star formation, as traced by [H II regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H_II_region "H II region")[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-TaylorCordes-219)[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-russeil03-220) and [molecular clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_cloud "Molecular cloud").[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-221)

The Milky Way's spiral structure is uncertain, and there is currently no consensus on the nature of the Milky Way's arms.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-two_arms-222) Perfect logarithmic spiral patterns only crudely describe features near the Sun,[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-russeil03-220)[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess09b-223) because galaxies commonly have arms that branch, merge, twist unexpectedly, and feature a degree of irregularity.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess10-193)[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess09b-223)[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-lepine10-224) The possible scenario of the Sun within a spur / Local arm[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-russeil03-220) emphasizes that point and indicates that such features are probably not unique, and exist elsewhere in the Milky Way.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-majaess09b-223) Estimates of the pitch angle of the arms range from about 7° to 25°.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-science312_1773-164)[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Drimmel2000-225) There are thought to be four spiral arms that all start near the Milky Way Galaxy's center.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-226) These are named as follows, with the positions of the arms shown in the image:

| Color | Arm(s) |
| --- | --- |
| turquoise | [Near 3 kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_3_kpc_Arm "Near 3 kpc Arm") and [Perseus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perseus_Arm "Perseus Arm") |
| blue | [Norma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norma_Arm "Norma Arm") and [Outer arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_arm "Outer arm") (Along with extension discovered in 2004[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mcclure-griffiths-227)) |
| green | [Far 3 kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_3_kpc_Arm "Far 3 kpc Arm") and [Scutum–Centaurus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scutum%E2%80%93Centaurus_Arm "Scutum–Centaurus Arm") |
| red | [Carina–Sagittarius Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carina%E2%80%93Sagittarius_Arm "Carina–Sagittarius Arm") |
| _There are at least two smaller arms or spurs, including:_ |
| orange | [Orion–Cygnus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion%E2%80%93Cygnus_Arm "Orion–Cygnus Arm") (which contains the Sun and Solar System) |

Two spiral arms, the Scutum–Centaurus arm and the Carina–Sagittarius arm, have tangent points inside the Sun's orbit about the center of the Milky Way. If these arms contain an overdensity of stars compared to the average density of stars in the Galactic disk, it would be detectable by counting the stars near the tangent point. Two surveys of near-infrared light, which is sensitive primarily to red giants and not affected by dust extinction, detected the predicted overabundance in the Scutum–Centaurus arm but not in the Carina–Sagittarius arm: the Scutum–Centaurus Arm contains approximately 30% more [red giants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") than would be expected in the absence of a spiral arm.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Drimmel2000-225)[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Benjamin2005-228)

This observation suggests that the Milky Way possesses only two major stellar arms: the Perseus arm and the Scutum–Centaurus arm.
