The rest of the arms contain excess gas but not excess old stars.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-two_arms-222) In December 2013, astronomers found that the distribution of young stars and star-forming regions matches the four-arm spiral description of the Milky Way.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-229)[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-230)[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-urquart2013-231) Thus, the Milky Way appears to have two spiral arms as traced by old stars and four spiral arms as traced by gas and young stars. The explanation for this apparent discrepancy is unclear.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-urquart2013-231)

The _[Near 3 kpc Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near\_3\_kpc\_Arm "Near 3 kpc Arm")_ (also called the _Expanding 3 kpc Arm_ or simply the _3 kpc Arm_) was discovered in the 1950s by astronomer van Woerden and collaborators through [21 centimeter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_line "Hydrogen line") radio measurements of H I ([atomic hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen "Atomic hydrogen")).[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-232)[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Dame-233) It was found to be expanding away from the central bulge at more than 50[km/s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilometer_per_second "Kilometer per second"). It is located in the fourth galactic quadrant at a distance of about 5.2[kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec "Parsec") from the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") and 3.3 kpc from the [Galactic Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_Center "Galactic Center"). The Far 3 kpc Arm was discovered in 2008 by astronomer Tom Dame ([Center for Astrophysics | Harvard & Smithsonian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Smithsonian_Center_for_Astrophysics "Harvard–Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics")). It is located in the first galactic quadrant at a distance of 3[kpc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec "Parsec") (about 10,000[ly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_year "Light year")) from the Galactic Center.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Dame-233)[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-234)

A simulation published in 2011 suggested that the Milky Way may have obtained its spiral arm structure as a result of repeated collisions with the [Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy "Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy").[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-235)

It has been suggested that the Milky Way contains two different spiral patterns: an inner one, formed by the Sagittarius arm, that rotates fast and an outer one, formed by the Carina and Perseus arms, whose rotation velocity is slower and whose arms are tightly wound. In this scenario, suggested by numerical simulations of the dynamics of the different spiral arms, the outer pattern would form an outer [pseudoring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification#De_Vaucouleurs_system "Galaxy morphological classification"),[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-pseudoring2-236) and the two patterns would be connected by the Cygnus arm.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-pseudoring-237)

Outside of the major spiral arms is the [Monoceros Ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monoceros_Ring "Monoceros Ring") (or Outer Ring), a ring of gas and stars torn from other galaxies billions of years ago. However, several members of the scientific community recently restated their position affirming the Monoceros structure is nothing more than an over-density produced by the flared and warped [thick disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thick_disk "Thick disk") of the Milky Way.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-238) The structure of the Milky Way's disk is warped along an ["S" curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigmoid_function "Sigmoid function").[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-239)

### Halo

The Galactic disk is surrounded by a [spheroidal halo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_spheroid "Galactic spheroid") of old stars and [globular clusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globular_cluster "Globular cluster"), of which 90% lie within 100,000 light-years (30 kpc) of the Galactic Center.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-harris2003-240) However, a few globular clusters have been found farther, such as PAL 4 and AM 1 at more than 200,000 light-years from the Galactic Center. About 40% of the Milky Way's clusters are on [retrograde orbits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retrograde_orbit "Retrograde orbit"), which means they move in the opposite direction from the Milky Way rotation.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-aaa313_119-241) The globular clusters can follow [rosette orbits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosette_orbit "Rosette orbit") about the Milky Way, in contrast to the [elliptical orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptical_orbit "Elliptical orbit") of a planet around a star.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj522-242)

Although the disk contains dust that obscures the view at some wavelengths, the halo component does not. Active [star formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_formation "Star formation") takes place in the disk (especially in the spiral arms, which represent areas of high density), but does not take place in the halo, as there is little cool gas to collapse into stars.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-sparke_gallagher-118)[Open clusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_cluster "Open cluster") are also located primarily on the disk.[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-aj108-243)

Discoveries in the early 21st century have added dimension to the knowledge of the Milky Way's structure. With the discovery that the disk of the Andromeda Galaxy (M31) extends much farther than previously thought,[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-fn5-244) the possibility of the disk of the Milky Way extending farther is apparent, and this is supported by evidence from the discovery of the Outer Arm extension of the [Cygnus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_Arm "Cygnus Arm")[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-mcclure-griffiths-227)[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-fn6-245) and of a similar extension of the [Scutum–Centaurus Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scutum%E2%80%93Centaurus_Arm "Scutum–Centaurus Arm").[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-246) With the discovery of the [Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy "Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy") came the discovery of a ribbon of galactic debris as the polar orbit of the dwarf and its interaction with the Milky Way tears it apart. Upon the 2004 discovery of a ring of galactic debris in an in-plane orbit around the Milky Way, it was initially believed that the debris was the remnant of a system dubbed the [Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canis_Major_Dwarf_Galaxy "Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy").[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-247) Other scholars believed it to be due to the Galactic warp,[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-248) a view which has been supported by more recent evidence as of 2021.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-249)

The [Sloan Digital Sky Survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey "Sloan Digital Sky Survey") of the northern sky shows a huge and diffuse structure (spread out across an area around 5,000 times the size of a full moon) within the Milky Way that does not seem to fit within current models. The collection of stars rises close to perpendicular to the plane of the spiral arms of the Milky Way. The proposed likely interpretation is that a [dwarf galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy "Dwarf galaxy") is merging with the Milky Way. This galaxy is tentatively named the [Virgo Stellar Stream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_Stellar_Stream "Virgo Stellar Stream") and is found in the direction of Virgo about 30,000 light-years (9 kpc) away.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj673_2_864-250)

#### Gaseous halo

In addition to the stellar halo, the [Chandra X-ray Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory "Chandra X-ray Observatory"), [XMM-Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/XMM-Newton "XMM-Newton"), and [_Suzaku_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suzaku_(satellite) "Suzaku (satellite)") have provided evidence that there is also a gaseous halo containing a large amount of hot gas. This halo extends for hundreds of thousands of light-years, much farther than the stellar halo and close to the distance of the Large and Small [Magellanic Clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds "Magellanic Clouds"). The mass of this hot halo is nearly equivalent to the mass of the Milky Way itself.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-chandra_hotgas-251)[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-252)[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-253) The temperature of this halo gas is between 1 and 2.5 million K (1.8 and 4.5 million°F).[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-254)

Observations of distant galaxies indicate that the Universe had about one-sixth as much [baryonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baryon "Baryon") (ordinary) matter as dark matter when it was just a few billion years old. However, only about half of those baryons are accounted for in the modern Universe based on observations of nearby galaxies like the Milky Way.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Harrington_feature-255) If the finding that the mass of the halo is comparable to the mass of the Milky Way is confirmed, it could be the identity of the missing baryons around the Milky Way.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Harrington_feature-255)

### Galactic rotation

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.svg/500px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rotation_curve_(Milky_Way).svg)

[Galaxy rotation curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve "Galaxy rotation curve") for the Milky Way – vertical axis is speed of rotation about the galactic center; horizontal axis is distance from the galactic center in kpcs; the sun is marked with a yellow ball; the observed curve of speed of rotation is blue; the predicted curve based upon stellar mass and gas in the Milky Way is red; scatter in observations roughly indicated by gray bars, the difference is due to dark matter.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Koupelis_Kuhn2007-256)[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Jones1-257) Note that close to the center the speed is actually proportional to the distance, going to zero at the center, as shown in a reference.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Schneider-258)

The stars and gas in the Milky Way rotate about its center [differentially](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_rotation "Differential rotation"), meaning that the rotation period varies with location. As is typical for spiral galaxies, the orbital speed of most stars in the Milky Way does not depend strongly on their distance from the center. Away from the central bulge or outer rim, the typical stellar orbital speed is between 200 and 220 km/s.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-259) Hence the [orbital period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_period "Orbital period") of the typical star is approximately proportional to the length of the path traveled. This is unlike the situation in the Solar System, where two-body gravitational dynamics dominate, and different orbits have significantly different velocities associated with them. The rotation curve (shown in the figure) describes this rotation.

If the Milky Way contained only the mass observed in stars, gas, and other baryonic (ordinary) matter, the rotational speed would decrease with distance from the center. However, the observed curve is relatively flat, indicating that there is additional mass that cannot be detected directly with electromagnetic radiation. This inconsistency is attributed to dark matter.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Koupelis_Kuhn2007-256) The rotation curve of the Milky Way agrees with the [universal rotation curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_rotation_curve "Universal rotation curve") of spiral galaxies, the best evidence for the existence of [dark matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter "Dark matter") in galaxies. Alternatively, a minority of astronomers propose that a [modification of the law of gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MOND "MOND") may explain the observed rotation curve.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Schneider_p413-260)

Formation
---------

### History

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Galaxy_color-magnitude_diagram-en.svg/250px-Galaxy_color-magnitude_diagram-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Galaxy_color-magnitude_diagram-en.svg)

A [galaxy color–magnitude diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_color%E2%80%93magnitude_diagram "Galaxy color–magnitude diagram") showing the red sequence (old galaxies, typically elliptical galaxies), the green valley (where the Milky Way is believed to be in), and the blue cloud (young galaxies, typically spiral galaxies).

The Milky Way began as one or several small overdensities in the mass distribution in the [Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe") shortly after the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang "Big Bang") 13.61 billion years ago.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-PHYS-20170727-261)[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-borah-262)[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-legassick-263) Some of these overdensities were the seeds of globular clusters in which the oldest remaining stars in what is now the Milky Way formed. Nearly half the matter in the Milky Way may have come from other distant galaxies.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-PHYS-20170727-261) These stars and clusters now comprise the stellar halo of the Milky Way. Within a few billion years of the birth of the first stars, the mass of the Milky Way was large enough so that it was spinning relatively quickly. Due to [conservation of angular momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum "Conservation of angular momentum"), this led the gaseous interstellar medium to collapse from a roughly spheroidal shape to a disk. Therefore, later generations of stars formed in this spiral disk. Most younger stars, including the Sun, are observed to be in the disk.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ut20090527-264)[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Buser-265)

Since the first stars began to form, the Milky Way has grown through both [galaxy mergers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_merger "Galaxy merger") (particularly early in the Milky Way's growth) and accretion of gas directly from the Galactic halo.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Buser-265) The Milky Way is currently accreting material from several small galaxies, including two of its largest satellite galaxies, the [Large](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud "Large Magellanic Cloud") and [Small](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud "Small Magellanic Cloud") Magellanic Clouds, through the [Magellanic Stream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magellanic_Stream "Magellanic Stream"). Direct accretion of gas is observed in [high-velocity clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-velocity_cloud "High-velocity cloud") like the [Smith Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smith_Cloud "Smith Cloud").[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-araa35_217-266)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj679_L21-267)

Cosmological simulations indicate that, 11 billion years ago, it merged with a particularly large galaxy that has been labeled the [Kraken](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kraken_galaxy "Kraken galaxy").[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-268)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-269) Properties of the Milky Way such as stellar mass, [angular momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_momentum "Angular momentum"), and [metallicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metallicity "Metallicity") in its outermost regions suggest it has undergone no mergers with large galaxies in the last 10 billion years. This lack of recent major mergers is unusual among similar spiral galaxies. Its neighbour the Andromeda Galaxy appears to have a more typical history shaped by more recent mergers with relatively large galaxies.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-270)[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-271)

According to recent studies, the Milky Way as well as the Andromeda Galaxy lie in what in the [galaxy color–magnitude diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_color%E2%80%93magnitude_diagram "Galaxy color–magnitude diagram") is known as the "green valley", a region populated by galaxies in transition from the "blue cloud" (galaxies actively forming new stars) to the "red sequence" (galaxies that lack star formation). Star-formation activity in green valley galaxies is slowing as they run out of star-forming gas in the interstellar medium. In simulated galaxies with similar properties, star formation will typically have been extinguished within about five billion years from now, even accounting for the expected, short-term increase in the rate of star formation due to the collision between both the Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-272) Measurements of other galaxies similar to the Milky Way suggest it is among the reddest and brightest spiral galaxies that are still forming new stars and it is just slightly bluer than the bluest red sequence galaxies.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-273)

### Age and cosmological history

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Early_Milky_Way.jpg/330px-Early_Milky_Way.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Early_Milky_Way.jpg)

Comparison of the night sky with the night sky of a hypothetical planet within the Milky Way 10 billion years ago, at an age of about 3.6 billion years and 5 billion years before the Sun formed.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-274)

Globular clusters are among the oldest objects in the Milky Way, which thus set a lower limit on the age of the Milky Way. The ages of individual stars in the Milky Way can be estimated by measuring the abundance of long-lived [radioactive elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_element "Radioactive element") such as [thorium-232](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thorium-232 "Thorium-232") and [uranium-238](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranium-238 "Uranium-238"), then comparing the results to estimates of their original abundance, a technique called [nucleocosmochronology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology "Nucleocosmochronology"). These yield values of about 12.5 ± 3 billion years for [CS 31082-001](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cayrel%27s_Star "Cayrel's Star")[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-275) and 13.8 ± 4 billion years for [BD +17° 3248](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BD_%2B17%C2%B0_3248 "BD +17° 3248").[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-276)

Once a [white dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_dwarf "White dwarf") is formed, it begins to undergo radiative cooling and the surface temperature steadily drops. By measuring the temperatures of the coolest of these white dwarfs and comparing them to their expected initial temperatures, an age estimate can be made. With this technique, the age of the globular cluster M4 was estimated as 12.7 ± 0.7 billion years. Age estimates of the oldest of these clusters give a best fit estimate of 12.6 billion years, and a 95% confidence upper limit of 16 billion years.[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-science299_5603_65-277)

In November 2018, astronomers reported the discovery of one of the oldest stars in the universe. About 13.5 billion-years-old, [2MASS J18082002-5104378 B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2MASS_J18082002-5104378_B "2MASS J18082002-5104378 B") is a tiny ultra metal-poor (UMP) star made almost entirely of materials released from the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang "Big Bang"), and is possibly one of the first stars. The discovery of the star in the Milky Way [Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy") suggests that the galaxy may be at least 3 billion years older than previously thought.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-EA-20181105-278)[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-JHU-20181105-279)[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-ApJ-20181105-280)

Several individual stars have been found in the Milky Way's halo with measured ages very close to the 13.80-billion-year [age of the Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe "Age of the Universe"). In 2007, a star in the galactic halo, [HE 1523-0901](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HE_1523-0901 "HE 1523-0901"), was estimated to be about 13.2 billion years old. As the oldest known object in the Milky Way at that time, this measurement placed a lower limit on the age of the Milky Way.[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-frebel-281) This estimate was made using the UV-Visual Echelle Spectrograph of the [Very Large Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope "Very Large Telescope") to [measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measurement "Measurement") the relative strengths of [spectral lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_line "Spectral line") caused by the presence of [thorium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thorium "Thorium") and other [elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical element") created by the [R-process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R-process "R-process"). The line strengths yield abundances of different elemental [isotopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope "Isotope"), from which an estimate of the age of the star can be derived using [nucleocosmochronology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology "Nucleocosmochronology").[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-frebel-281) Another star, [HD 140283](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HD_140283 "HD 140283"), has been estimated at either 13.7 ± 0.7 billion years, 12.2 ± 0.6 billion years,[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-creevey-282) or 12.0 ± 0.5 billion years.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Tang-283)

According to observations utilizing [adaptive optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adaptive_optics "Adaptive optics") to correct for Earth's atmospheric distortion, stars in the galaxy's bulge date to about 12.8 billion years old.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-284)

The age of stars in the galactic [thin disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thin_disk "Thin disk") has also been estimated using nucleocosmochronology. Measurements of thin disk stars yield an estimate that the thin disk formed 8.8 ± 1.7 billion years ago. These measurements suggest there was a hiatus of almost 5 billion years between the formation of the [galactic halo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactic_halo "Galactic halo") and the thin disk.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-del_Peloso-285) Recent analysis of the chemical signatures of thousands of stars suggests that stellar formation might have dropped by an order of magnitude at the time of disk formation, 10 to 8 billion years ago, when interstellar gas was too hot to form new stars at the same rate as before.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-286)

The satellite galaxies surrounding the Milky Way are not randomly distributed but seem to be the result of a breakup of some larger system producing a ring structure 500,000 light-years in diameter and 50,000 light-years wide.[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-287) Close encounters between galaxies, like that expected in 4 billion years with the Andromeda Galaxy, can rip off huge tails of gas, which, over time can coalesce to form dwarf galaxies in a ring at an arbitrary angle to the main disc.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-288)

Intergalactic neighbourhood
---------------------------

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png/250px-06-Local_Group_%28LofE06240%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:06-Local_Group_(LofE06240).png)

A diagram of the galaxies in the [Local Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Group "Local Group") relative to the Milky Way

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png/250px-07-Laniakea_%28LofE07240%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:07-Laniakea_(LofE07240).png)

The Milky Way and the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy") are a [binary system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy) "Binary system (astronomy)") of giant spiral galaxies belonging to a group of 50 closely bound galaxies known as the [Local Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Group "Local Group"), surrounded by a Local Void, itself being part of the [Local Sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Sheet "Local Sheet")[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-apj676_1_184-289) and in turn the [Virgo Supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster "Virgo Supercluster"). Surrounding the Virgo Supercluster are a number of [voids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Void_(astronomy) "Void (astronomy)"), devoid of many galaxies, the Microscopium Void to the "north", the Sculptor Void to the "left", the [Boötes Void](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bo%C3%B6tes_Void "Boötes Void") to the "right" and the Canes-Major Void to the "south". These voids change shape over time, creating filamentous structures of galaxies. The Virgo Supercluster, for instance, is being drawn towards the [Great Attractor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Attractor "Great Attractor"),[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-290) which in turn forms part of a greater structure, called [Laniakea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster "Laniakea Supercluster").[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-291)

Two smaller galaxies and a number of [dwarf galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy "Dwarf galaxy") in the Local Group orbit the Milky Way. The largest of these is the [Large Magellanic Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud "Large Magellanic Cloud") with a diameter of 32,200 light-years.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-RC3-292) It has a close companion, the [Small Magellanic Cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud "Small Magellanic Cloud"). The [Magellanic Stream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magellanic_Stream "Magellanic Stream") is a stream of neutral [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") gas extending from these two small galaxies across 100° of the sky. The stream is thought to have been dragged from the Magellanic Clouds in tidal interactions with the Milky Way.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-293) Some of the [dwarf galaxies orbiting the Milky Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way%27s_satellite_galaxies "Milky Way's satellite galaxies") are [Canis Major Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canis_Major_Dwarf "Canis Major Dwarf") (the closest), [Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy "Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy"), [Ursa Minor Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ursa_Minor_Dwarf "Ursa Minor Dwarf"), [Sculptor Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sculptor_Dwarf "Sculptor Dwarf"), [Sextans Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sextans_Dwarf "Sextans Dwarf"), [Fornax Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fornax_Dwarf "Fornax Dwarf"), and [Leo I Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leo_I_Dwarf "Leo I Dwarf").[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Koposov2015-294)

The smallest dwarf galaxies of the Milky Way are only 500 light-years in diameter. These include [Carina Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carina_Dwarf "Carina Dwarf"), [Draco Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Draco_Dwarf "Draco Dwarf"), and [Leo II Dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leo_II_(dwarf_galaxy) "Leo II (dwarf galaxy)"). There may still be undetected dwarf galaxies that are dynamically bound to the Milky Way, which is supported by the detection of nine new satellites of the Milky Way in a relatively small patch of the night sky in 2015.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Koposov2015-294) There are some dwarf galaxies that have already been absorbed by the Milky Way, such as the progenitor of [Omega Centauri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omega_Centauri "Omega Centauri").[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-295)

In 2005[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-296) with further confirmation in 2012[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-297) researchers reported that most satellite galaxies of the Milky Way lie in a very large disk and orbit in the same direction. This came as a surprise: according to standard cosmology, satellite galaxies should form in dark matter halos, and they should be widely distributed and moving in random directions. This discrepancy is still not explained.[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-298)

In January 2006, researchers reported that the heretofore unexplained warp in the disk of the Milky Way has now been mapped and found to be a ripple or vibration set up by the Large and Small Magellanic Clouds as they orbit the Milky Way, causing vibrations when they pass through its edges. Previously, these two galaxies, at around 2% of the mass of the Milky Way, were considered too small to influence the Milky Way. However, in a computer model, the movement of these two galaxies creates a dark matter wake that amplifies their influence on the larger Milky Way.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-berkeley20060109-299)

Current measurements suggest the Andromeda Galaxy is approaching the Milky Way at 100 to 140 km/s (220,000 to 310,000 mph). In 4.3 billion years, there may be an [Andromeda–Milky Way collision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda%E2%80%93Milky_Way_collision "Andromeda–Milky Way collision"), depending on the importance of unknown lateral components to the galaxies' relative motion. If they collide, the chance of individual [stars colliding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_collision "Stellar collision") with each other is extremely low,[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-wong20000414-300) but instead the two galaxies will merge to form a single [elliptical galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy "Elliptical galaxy") or perhaps a large [disk galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disk_galaxy "Disk galaxy")[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Ueda2014-301) over the course of about six billion years.[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-Schiavi-302)

Velocity
--------

Although [special relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_relativity "Special relativity") states that there is no "preferred" [inertial frame of reference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference "Inertial frame of reference") in space with which to compare the Milky Way, the Milky Way does have a velocity with respect to cosmological [frames of reference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frames_of_reference "Frames of reference").[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-303)

One such frame of reference is the [Hubble flow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_flow "Hubble flow"), the apparent motions of galaxy clusters due to the [expansion of space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expansion_of_space "Expansion of space"). Individual galaxies, including the Milky Way, have [peculiar velocities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peculiar_velocities "Peculiar velocities") relative to the average flow. Thus, to compare the Milky Way to the Hubble flow, one must consider a volume large enough so that the expansion of the Universe dominates over local, random motions. A large enough volume means that the mean motion of galaxies within this volume is equal to the Hubble flow. Astronomers believe the Milky Way is moving at approximately 630 km/s (1,400,000 mph) with respect to this local co-moving frame of reference.[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-304)[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-305)

The Milky Way is moving in the general direction of the [Great Attractor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Attractor "Great Attractor") and other [galaxy clusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_cluster "Galaxy cluster"), including the [Shapley Supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shapley_Supercluster "Shapley Supercluster"), behind it.[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-306) The Local Group, a cluster of gravitationally bound galaxies containing, among others, the Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy, is part of a [supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercluster "Supercluster") called the [Local Supercluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_Supercluster "Local Supercluster"), centered near the [Virgo Cluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_Cluster "Virgo Cluster"): although they are moving away from each other at 967 km/s (2,160,000 mph) as part of the Hubble flow, this velocity is less than would be expected given the 16.8 million pc distance due to the gravitational attraction between the Local Group and the Virgo Cluster.[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-307)

Another reference frame is provided by the [cosmic microwave background](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background "Cosmic microwave background") (CMB), in which the CMB temperature is least distorted by Doppler shift (zero dipole moment). The Milky Way is moving at 552±6 km/s (1,235,000±13,000 mph)[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-COBE1993-23) with respect to this frame, toward 10.5 right ascension, −24° declination ([J2000](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J2000 "J2000") epoch, near the center of [Hydra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydra_(constellation) "Hydra (constellation)")). This motion is observed by satellites such as the [Cosmic Background Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer "Cosmic Background Explorer") (COBE) and the [Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe "Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe") (WMAP) as a dipole contribution to the CMB, as photons in equilibrium in the CMB frame get [blue-shifted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_effect "Doppler effect") in the direction of the motion and [red-shifted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redshift "Redshift") in the opposite direction.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_note-COBE1993-23)

83.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_ref-88)**Thomas Wright, _An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe_ (London, England: H. Chapelle, 1750). 
    *   [On page 57](https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA57)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120195004/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA57) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"), Wright stated that despite their mutual gravitational attraction, the stars in the constellations do not collide because they are in orbit, so centrifugal force keeps them separated: "centrifugal force, which not only preserves them in their orbits, but prevents them from rushing all together, by the common universal law of gravity,..."
    *   [On page 48](https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA48)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120194825/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA48) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"), Wright stated that the form of the Milky Way is a ring: "the stars are not infinitely dispersed and distributed in a promiscuous manner throughout all the mundane space, without order or design, ... this phænomenon [is] no other than a certain effect arising from the observer's situation, ... To a spectator placed in an indefinite space, ... it [i.e. the Milky Way (_Via Lactea_)] [is] a vast ring of stars..."
    *   [On page 65](https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA65)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120174208/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA65) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"), Wright speculated that the central body of the Milky Way, around which the rest of the galaxy revolves, might not be visible to us: "the central body A, being supposed as _incognitum_ [i.e. an unknown], without [i.e. outside of] the finite view;..."
    *   [On page 73](https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA73)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120194830/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA73) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"), Wright called the Milky Way the _Vortex Magnus_ (the great whirlpool) and estimated its diameter to be 8.64×10 12 miles (13.9×10 12 km).
    *   [On page 33](https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA33)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120172753/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&pg=PA33) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"), Wright speculated that there are a vast number of inhabited planets in the galaxy: "therefore we may justly suppose, that so many radiant bodies [i.e. stars] were not created barely to enlighten an infinite void, but to... display an infinite shapeless universe, crowded with myriads of glorious worlds, all variously revolving round them; and... with an inconceivable variety of beings and states, animate..."

84.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_ref-89)**Immanuel Kant, [_Allgemeine Naturgeschichte und Theorie des Himmels_](https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&pg=PP9)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120195036/https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&pg=PP9) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine") [_General Natural History and Theory of Heaven_], (Koenigsberg and Leipzig, (Germany): Johann Friederich Petersen, 1755). On pages 2–3, Kant acknowledged his debt to Thomas Wright: _"Dem Herrn Wright von Durham, einen Engeländer, war es vorbehalten, einen glücklichen Schritt zu einer Bemerkung zu thun, welche von ihm selber zu keiner gar zu tüchtigen Absicht gebraucht zu seyn scheinet, und deren nützliche Anwendung er nicht genugsam beobachtet hat. Er betrachtete die Fixsterne nicht als ein ungeordnetes und ohne Absicht zerstreutes Gewimmel, sondern er fand eine systematische Verfassung im Ganzen, und eine allgemeine Beziehung dieser Gestirne gegen einen Hauptplan der Raume, die sie einnehmen."_ ("To Mr. Wright of Durham, an Englishman, it was reserved to take a happy step towards an observation, which seemed, to him and to no one else, to be needed for a clever idea, the exploitation of which he has not studied sufficiently. He regarded the fixed stars not as a disorganized swarm that was scattered without a design; rather, he found a systematic shape in the whole, and a general relation between these stars and the principal plane of the space that they occupy.")
85.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_ref-90)**Kant (1755), [pages xxxiii–xxxvi of the Preface (_Vorrede_)](https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&pg=PP49): [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120231151/https://books.google.com/books?id=nCcaAQAAMAAJ&pg=PP49) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine"): _"Ich betrachtete die Art neblichter Sterne, deren Herr von Maupertuis in der Abhandlung von der Figur der Gestirne gedenket, und die die Figur von mehr oder weniger offenen Ellipsen vorstellen, und versicherte mich leicht, daß sie nichts anders als eine Häufung vieler Fixsterne seyn können. Die jederzeit abgemessene Rundung dieser Figuren belehrte mich, daß hier ein unbegreiflich zahlreiches Sternenheer, und zwar um einen gemeinschaftlichen Mittelpunkt, müste geordnet seyn, weil sonst ihre freye Stellungen gegen einander, wohl irreguläre Gestalten, aber nicht abgemessene Figuren vorstellen würden. Ich sahe auch ein: daß sie in dem System, darinn sie sich vereinigt befinden, vornemlich auf eine Fläche beschränkt seyn müßten, weil sie nicht zirkelrunde, sondern elliptische Figuren abbilden, und daß sie wegen ihres blassen Lichts unbegreiflich weit von uns abstehen."_ ("I considered the type of nebulous stars, which Mr. de Maupertuis considered in his treatise on the shape of stars, and which present the figures of more or less open ellipses, and I readily assured myself, that they could be nothing else than a cluster of fixed stars. That these figures always measured round informed me that here an inconceivably numerous host of stars, [which were clustered] around a common center, must be orderly, because otherwise their free positions among each other would probably present irregular forms, not measurable figures. I also realized: that in the system in which they find themselves bound, they must be restricted primarily to a plane, because they display not circular, but elliptical figures, and that on account of their faint light, they are located inconceivably far from us.")
86.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_ref-our_galaxy_91-0)**Evans, J. C. (November 24, 1998). ["Our Galaxy"](http://physics.gmu.edu/~jevans/astr103/CourseNotes/ECText/ch20_txt.htm). George Mason University. [Archived](https://archive.today/20120630/http://physics.gmu.edu/~jevans/astr103/CourseNotes/ECText/ch20_txt.htm) from the original on June 30, 2012. Retrieved January 4, 2007.
87.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way#cite_ref-92)**The term _Weltinsel_ (world island) appears nowhere in Kant's book of 1755. The term first appeared in 1850, in the third volume of von Humboldt's _Kosmos_: Alexander von Humboldt, _Kosmos_, vol. 3 (Stuttgart & Tübingen, (Germany): J. G. Cotta, 1850), pp. 187, 189. [From p. 187](https://books.google.com/books?id=Su0iAQAAMAAJ&pg=PA187): [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20161120174304/https://books.google.com/books?id=Su0iAQAAMAAJ&pg=PA187) November 20, 2016, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine")_"Thomas Wright von Durham, Kant, Lambert und zuerst auch William Herschel waren geneigt die Gestalt der Milchstraße und die scheinbare Anhäufung der Sterne in derselben als eine Folge der abgeplatteten Gestalt und ungleichen Dimensionen der_ Weltinsel _(Sternschict) zu betrachten, in welche unser Sonnensystem eingeschlossen ist."_ ("Thomas Wright of Durham, Kant, Lambert and at first also William Herschel were inclined to regard the shape of the Milky Way and the apparent clustering of stars in it as a consequence of the oblate shape and unequal dimensions of the _world island_ (star stratum), in which our solar system is included.)

 In the English translation– Alexander von Humboldt with [E. C. Otté](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E._C._Ott%C3%A9 "E. C. Otté"), trans., _Cosmos_ ... (New York City: Harper & Brothers, 1897), vols. 3–5. see [p. 147](http://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=njp.32101057350447;view=1up;seq=157)[Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20181106181344/https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=njp.32101057350447;view=1up;seq=157) November 6, 2018, at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine").
